<meta name="DC.Title" content="Genome of Crucihimalaya himalaica, a close relative of Arabidopsis, shows ecological adaptation to high altitude" />
<meta name="DC.Identifier" content="10.1073/pnas.1817580116" />
<meta name="DC.Date" content="2019-04-02" />
<meta name="DC.Publisher" content="National Academy of Sciences" />
<meta name="DC.Rights" content="Copyright © 2019 the Author(s). Published by PNAS.. https://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc-nd/4.0/This open access article is distributed under Creative Commons Attribution-NonCommercial-NoDerivatives License 4.0 (CC BY-NC-ND)." />
<meta name="DC.AccessRights" content="open-access" />
<meta name="DC.Description" content="Crucihimalaya himalaica is a close relative of Arabidopsis with typical Qinghai–Tibet Plateau (QTP) distribution. Here, by combining short- and long-read sequencing technologies, we provide a de novo genome sequence of C. himalaica . Our results suggest that the quick uplifting of the QTP coincided with the expansion of repeat elements. Gene families showing dramatic contractions and expansions, as well as genes showing clear signs of natural selection, were likely responsible for C. himalaica ’s specific adaptation to the harsh environment of the QTP. We also show that the transition to self-pollination of C. himalaica might have enabled its occupation of the QTP. This study provides insights into how plants might adapt to extreme environmental conditions." />

<meta name="article:section" content="PNAS Plus" />
<meta name="citation_title" content="Genome of Crucihimalaya himalaica, a close relative of Arabidopsis, shows ecological adaptation to high altitude" />
<meta name="citation_abstract" lang="en" content="&lt;p&gt;&lt;i&gt;Crucihimalaya himalaica&lt;/i&gt;, a close relative of &lt;i&gt;Arabidopsis&lt;/i&gt; and &lt;i&gt;Capsella&lt;/i&gt;, grows on the Qinghai–Tibet Plateau (QTP) about 4,000 m above sea level and represents an attractive model system for studying speciation and ecological adaptation in extreme environments. We assembled a draft genome sequence of 234.72 Mb encoding 27,019 genes and investigated its origin and adaptive evolutionary mechanisms. Phylogenomic analyses based on 4,586 single-copy genes revealed that &lt;i&gt;C. himalaica&lt;/i&gt; is most closely related to &lt;i&gt;Capsella&lt;/i&gt; (estimated divergence 8.8 to 12.2 Mya), whereas both species form a sister clade to &lt;i&gt;Arabidopsis thaliana&lt;/i&gt; and &lt;i&gt;Arabidopsis lyrata&lt;/i&gt;, from which they diverged between 12.7 and 17.2 Mya. LTR retrotransposons in &lt;i&gt;C. himalaica&lt;/i&gt; proliferated shortly after the dramatic uplift and climatic change of the Himalayas from the Late Pliocene to Pleistocene. Compared with closely related species, &lt;i&gt;C. himalaica&lt;/i&gt; showed significant contraction and pseudogenization in gene families associated with disease resistance and also significant expansion in gene families associated with ubiquitin-mediated proteolysis and DNA repair. We identified hundreds of genes involved in DNA repair, ubiquitin-mediated proteolysis, and reproductive processes with signs of positive selection. Gene families showing dramatic changes in size and genes showing signs of positive selection are likely candidates for &lt;i&gt;C. himalaica&lt;/i&gt;’s adaptation to intense radiation, low temperature, and pathogen-depauperate environments in the QTP. Loss of function at the S-locus, the reason for the transition to self-fertilization of &lt;i&gt;C. himalaica&lt;/i&gt;, might have enabled its QTP occupation. Overall, the genome sequence of &lt;i&gt;C. himalaica&lt;/i&gt; provides insights into the mechanisms of plant adaptation to extreme environments.&lt;/p&gt;" />
<meta name="citation_abstract" lang="en" scheme="executive-summary" content="&lt;h3&gt;Significance&lt;/h3&gt;
&lt;p&gt;&lt;i&gt;Crucihimalaya himalaica&lt;/i&gt; is a close relative of &lt;i&gt;Arabidopsis&lt;/i&gt; with typical Qinghai–Tibet Plateau (QTP) distribution. Here, by combining short- and long-read sequencing technologies, we provide a de novo genome sequence of &lt;i&gt;C. himalaica&lt;/i&gt;. Our results suggest that the quick uplifting of the QTP coincided with the expansion of repeat elements. Gene families showing dramatic contractions and expansions, as well as genes showing clear signs of natural selection, were likely responsible for &lt;i&gt;C. himalaica&lt;/i&gt;’s specific adaptation to the harsh environment of the QTP. We also show that the transition to self-pollination of &lt;i&gt;C. himalaica&lt;/i&gt; might have enabled its occupation of the QTP. This study provides insights into how plants might adapt to extreme environmental conditions.&lt;/p&gt;" />

<meta name="twitter:description" content="Crucihimalaya himalaica is a close relative of Arabidopsis with typical Qinghai–Tibet Plateau (QTP) distribution. Here, by combining short- and long-read sequencing technologies, we provide a de novo genome sequence of C. himalaica . Our results suggest that the quick uplifting of the QTP coincided with the expansion of repeat elements. Gene families showing dramatic contractions and expansions, as well as genes showing clear signs of natural selection, were likely responsible for C. himalaica ’s specific adaptation to the harsh environment of the QTP. We also show that the transition to self-pollination of C. himalaica might have enabled its occupation of the QTP. This study provides insights into how plants might adapt to extreme environmental conditions." />

Here, by combining short- and long-read sequencing technologies, we provide a de novo genome sequence of C. himalaica . Our results suggest that the quick uplifting of the QTP coincided with the expansion of repeat elements. Gene families showing dramatic contractions and expansions, as well as genes showing clear signs of natural selection, were likely responsible for C. himalaica ’s specific adaptation to the harsh environment of the QTP. We also show that the transition to self-pollination of C. himalaica might have enabled its occupation of the QTP. This study provides insights into how plants might adapt to extreme environmental conditions." />

      <h1 class="highwire-cite-title" id="page-title">Genome of <em>Crucihimalaya himalaica</em>, a close relative of <em>Arabidopsis</em>, shows ecological adaptation to high altitude</h1>  
    	<div class="highwire-cite-authors"><span  class="highwire-citation-authors"><span class="highwire-citation-author first" data-delta="0">Ticao Zhang</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="1">Qin Qiao</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="2">Polina Yu. Al-Shehbaz</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="11">Hang Sun</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="12">Marc Van Montagu</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="13">Jinling Huang</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author hw-author-orcid-logo-wrapper" data-delta="14"><a href="http://orcid.org/0000-0003-4327-3730" target="_blank" class="hw-author-orcid-logo link-icon-only link-icon"><i class="hw-icon-orcid hw-icon-color-orcid"></i> <span class="title element-invisible">View ORCID Profile</span></a>Yves Van de Peer</span>, and <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="15">La Qiong</span></span></div>

<div class="panel-separator"></div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-panel-tabs-container" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div data-panels-ajax-tab-preloaded="jnl_pnas_tab_art" id="panels-ajax-tab-container-highwire_article_tabs" class="panels-ajax-tab-container"><div class="panels-ajax-tab-loading" style ="display:none"><img class="loading" src="https://www.pnas.org/sites/all/modules/contrib/panels_ajax_tab/images/loading.gif" alt="Loading" title="Loading" /></div><div class="panels-ajax-tab-wrap-jnl_pnas_tab_art"><div class="panel-display panel-1col clearfix" >
  <div class="panel-panel panel-col">
    <div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-markup" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="highwire-markup"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="content-block-markup" data-highwire-cite-ref-tooltip-instance="highwire_reflinks_tooltip" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><div class="article fulltext-view "><span class="highwire-journal-article-marker-start"></span><div class="executive-summary"><h2 class="">Significance</h2><p id="p-6"><em>Crucihimalaya himalaica</em> is a close relative of <em>Arabidopsis</em> with typical Qinghai–Tibet Plateau (QTP) distribution. Here, by combining short- and long-read sequencing technologies, we provide a de novo genome sequence of <em>C. himalaica</em>. Our results suggest that the quick uplifting of the QTP coincided with the expansion of repeat elements. Gene families showing dramatic contractions and expansions, as well as genes showing clear signs of natural selection, were likely responsible for <em>C. himalaica</em>’s specific adaptation to the harsh environment of the QTP. We also show that the transition to self-pollination of <em>C. himalaica</em> might have enabled its occupation of the QTP. This study provides insights into how plants might adapt to extreme environmental conditions.</p></div><div class="section abstract" id="abstract-2"><h2>Abstract</h2><p id="p-7"><em>Crucihimalaya himalaica</em>, a close relative of <em>Arabidopsis</em> and <em>Capsella</em>, grows on the Qinghai–Tibet Plateau (QTP) about 4,000 m above sea level and represents an attractive model system for studying speciation and ecological adaptation in extreme environments. We assembled a draft genome sequence of 234.72 Mb encoding 27,019 genes and investigated its origin and adaptive evolutionary mechanisms. Phylogenomic analyses based on 4,586 single-copy genes revealed that <em>C. himalaica</em> is most closely related to <em>Capsella</em> (estimated divergence 8.8 to 12.2 Mya), whereas both species form a sister clade to <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em> and <em>Arabidopsis lyrata</em>, from which they diverged between 12.7 and 17.2 Mya. LTR retrotransposons in <em>C. himalaica</em> proliferated shortly after the dramatic uplift and climatic change of the Himalayas from the Late Pliocene to Pleistocene. Compared with closely related species, <em>C. himalaica</em> showed significant contraction and pseudogenization in gene families associated with disease resistance and also significant expansion in gene families associated with ubiquitin-mediated proteolysis and DNA repair. We identified hundreds of genes involved in DNA repair, ubiquitin-mediated proteolysis, and reproductive processes with signs of positive selection. Gene families showing dramatic changes in size and genes showing signs of positive selection are likely candidates for <em>C. himalaica</em>’s adaptation to intense radiation, low temperature, and pathogen-depauperate environments in the QTP. Loss of function at the S-locus, the reason for the transition to self-fertilization of <em>C. himalaica</em>, might have enabled its QTP occupation. Overall, the genome sequence of <em>C. himalaica</em> provides insights into the mechanisms of plant adaptation to extreme environments.</p></div><ul class="kwd-group"><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/adaptive-evolution" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-adaptive-evolution" rel="nofollow">adaptive evolution</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/natural-selection" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-natural-selection" rel="nofollow">natural selection</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/extreme-environment" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-extreme-environment" rel="nofollow">extreme environment</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/qinghai%E2%80%93tibet-plateau" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-qinghai&#x2013;tibet-plateau" rel="nofollow">Qinghai–Tibet Plateau</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/s-locus" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-s-locus" rel="nofollow">S-locus</a></li></ul><p id="p-8" class="flushleft">The Qinghai–Tibet Plateau (QTP), also known as Himalayan Plateau, is the highest (average elevation above 4,000 m) and largest (<em>ca</em>. 2.5 million km<sup>2</sup>) plateau in the world. The QTP mountains reached their current elevations mainly between the late Miocene and late Pliocene, according to recent syntheses of different studies (<a id="xref-ref-1-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1">1</a>). The uplift of the QTP resulted in profound climatic and environmental changes in both the plateau region and Asia at large (<a id="xref-ref-2-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">2</a>). Conditions on the QTP are now characterized by low temperature, low oxygen, reduced pathogen incidence, and strong UV radiation, which together provide a unique environment to study adaptive evolution (<a id="xref-ref-3-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-3">3</a>, <a id="xref-ref-4-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4">4</a>). Previous genome-wide studies on adaptive evolution on the QTP have focused mainly on humans and vertebrates (reviewed in ref. <a id="xref-ref-5-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5">5</a>). These studies have revealed that genes involved in hypoxia responses, energy metabolism, and skeletal development are under positive selection and rapid evolution. In contrast, hitherto, no study of adaptive evolution based on whole-genome analysis has been conducted on wild plants (excluding the domesticated Tibetan highland barley) (<a id="xref-ref-6-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-6">6</a>) in this region.</p><p id="p-9"><em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em> (Cruciferae/Brassicaceae) is the best-known model organism in plant biology. Relatives of <em>A. thaliana</em> are important for studies on evolutionary ecology and comparative genomics of plants (<a id="xref-ref-7-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7">7</a><a id="xref-ref-8-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-8"><span>⇓</span></a>–<a id="xref-ref-9-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-9">9</a>), especially those found in specific and challenging environments. <em>Crucihimalaya himalaica</em> (Edgew.) Al-Shehbaz, O’Kane &amp; R.A.Price, a self-fertilizing, diploid (2n = 16) relative of <em>A. thaliana</em>, was previously recognized as <em>A. himalaica</em> (Edgew.) O.E.Schulz, and the names <em>Arabis rupestris</em> Edgew. and <em>Arabis brevicaulis</em> Jafri are also accepted as synonyms (<a id="xref-ref-10-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-10">10</a>). <em>C. himalaica</em> mainly grows on rocky hillsides, sandy slopes, alpine meadows, and screes in the Himalayas and Hengduan Mountains. Previous estimates suggested that <em>Crucihimalaya</em> diverged from the closely related <em>Pachycladon</em> ∼10.94 Mya (<a id="xref-ref-11-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11">11</a>), while the species <em>C. himalaica</em> originated about 3.56 Mya (<a id="xref-ref-12-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-12">12</a>). Given the postulated timing of the most recent rapid uplift of the QTP during the period of late Miocene to late Pliocene (<a id="xref-ref-1-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1">1</a>, <a id="xref-ref-13-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-13">13</a>), many of the genetic changes in <em>C. himalaica</em> might reflect its adaptation to the extreme environment of QTP (<a id="xref-ref-4-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4">4</a>).</p><p id="p-10">In a previous study (<a id="xref-ref-4-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4">4</a>), we identified 487 positively selected genes (PSGs) in the transcriptome of <em>C. himalaica</em>. Predicted functions of these PSGs indicate that they potentially contribute to miscellaneous traits of adaptive importance, such as response to UV radiation, DNA repair, and membrane stabilization, which presumably are important for the adaptation of <em>C. himalaica</em> to the specialized environment on the QTP. Therefore, we believe that this species represents a model system for the study of speciation and ecological adaptation in extreme environments. In the present study, we generated de novo whole-genome sequences of <em>C. himalaica</em> and applied comparative and evolutionary genomics approaches to clarify the origin of this species and to investigate signals of adaptation. Our goal is to gain a deeper understanding into the mechanisms by which <em>C. himalaica</em> has adapted to the complex extreme conditions on the QTP at the whole-genome level.</p><div class="section results-discussion" id="sec-1"><h2 class="">Results and Discussion</h2><div id="sec-2" class="subsection"><h3>Genome Assembly and Annotation.</h3><p id="p-11">The genome size of <em>C. himalaica</em> was estimated to be 265.23 Mb with a heterozygosity of 0.70% on the basis of <em>k</em>-mer statistics (<a id="xref-ref-14-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-14">14</a>) (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1817580116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S1</a>). In total, we generated 50.68 Gb of paired-end (PE) and mate-pair (MP) reads with different insert sizes using an Illumina HiSeq platform combined with PacBio sequencing technology (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1817580116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S2</a>). A total assembly of 234.72Mb, consisting of 583 scaffolds (scaffold N50 length, 2.09 Mb; longest scaffold, 8.34 Mb), was achieved by combination of the ALLPATHS–LG (<a id="xref-ref-15-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-15">15</a>) and PBJelly2 (<a id="xref-ref-16-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-16">16</a>) assembly strategies (<a id="xref-table-wrap-1-1" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table 1</a>). To assess assembly accuracy, we remapped raw sequencing reads of a small fragment library to the assembled genome. With a 96.69% mapping rate and 224.57× average sequence depth, the reads covered 96.43% of the genome, which implies that the current assembly covered almost all unique genomic regions. About 94.05% of the assembly was covered by at least 20× reads, which guaranteed a highly accurate assembly at the single-nucleotide level (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1817580116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S3</a>).</p><div id="T1" class="table pos-float"><div class="table-inline table-callout-links"><div class="callout"><span>View this table:</span><ul class="callout-links"><li class="view-inline first"><a href="##" class="table-expand-inline" data-table-url="/highwire/markup/857687/expansion?postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed&amp;table-expand-inline=1" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View inline</a></li>
<li class="view-popup last"><a href="/highwire/markup/857687/expansion?width=1000&amp;height=500&amp;iframe=true&amp;postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed" class="colorbox colorbox-load table-expand-popup" rel="gallery-fragment-tables" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View popup</a></li>
</ul></div></div><div class="table-caption"><span class="table-label">Table 1.</span> <p id="p-12" class="first-child">Genome assembly of <em>C. himalaica</em></p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-13">A total of 27,019 protein-coding genes were predicted (<a id="xref-table-wrap-1-2" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table 1</a> and <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1817580116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S4</a>). Among these, 26,806 genes (99.21%) were functionally annotated (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1817580116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S5</a>). In addition to protein-coding genes, various noncoding RNA sequences were identified and annotated (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1817580116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S6</a>), including 448 microRNAs, 577 transfer RNAs, 153 ribosomal RNAs, and 974 small nuclear RNAs. Gene region completeness was evaluated by RNA sequencing data (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1817580116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S7</a>). Of the 29,420 transcripts assembled by Trinity, 99.74% were mapped to the genome assembly, and 92.37% were complete. The completeness of gene regions was further assessed using BUSCO (Benchmarking Universal Single Copy Orthologs) (<a id="xref-ref-17-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17">17</a>), which showed that 96% of the plant single-copy orthologs were complete (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1817580116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S8</a>). Compared with other close relatives, the <em>C. himalaica</em> genome contains a similar number of transcription factors (1,711) and transcription regulators (413) (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1817580116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S9</a>). The <em>C. himalaica</em> genome showed strong homology with the genome of <em>A. thaliana</em>, <em>Arabidopsis lyrata</em>, and <em>Capsella rubella</em>, except in centromeres and certain high-repeat regions (<a id="xref-fig-1-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1</a> and <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1817580116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Figs. S1–S3</a>). These results implied that the assembly was of high quality, thus ensuring the reliability of subsequent comparative genomic analyses in this study.</p><div id="F1" class="fig pos-float type-featured  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/14/7137/F1.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Comparative analyses of genomic features of C. himalaica vs. A. thaliana (A) and C. himalaica vs. C. rubella (B). Tracks from inside to outside are collinearity between both genomes, number of chromosomes/scaffolds, gene density, and TE density." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-1204342201" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Comparative analyses of genomic features of &lt;em&gt;C. himalaica&lt;/em&gt; vs. &lt;em&gt;A. thaliana&lt;/em&gt; (&lt;em&gt;A&lt;/em&gt;) and &lt;em&gt;C. himalaica&lt;/em&gt; vs. &lt;em&gt;C. rubella&lt;/em&gt; (&lt;em&gt;B&lt;/em&gt;). Tracks from inside to outside are collinearity between both genomes, number of chromosomes/scaffolds, gene density, and TE density.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. 1." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/14/7137/F1.medium.gif" width="440" height="218"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. 1." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/14/7137/F1.medium.gif" width="440" height="218"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/14/7137/F1.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. 1." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>

</ul></div><div class="fig-caption" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 1.</span> <p id="p-14" class="first-child">Comparative analyses of genomic features of <em>C. himalaica</em> vs. <em>A. thaliana</em> (<em>A</em>) and <em>C. himalaica</em> vs. <em>C. rubella</em> (<em>B</em>). Tracks from inside to outside are collinearity between both genomes, number of chromosomes/scaffolds, gene density, and TE density.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div><div id="sec-3" class="subsection"><h3>Repeat Elements.</h3><p id="p-15">Compared with the genome size of closely related species, such as <em>C. rubella</em> (which has an assembled genome size of 134.8 Mb, whereas the genome size estimated from flow cytometry was 219 Mb) (<a id="xref-ref-18-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-18">18</a>) and <em>A. thaliana</em> [125 Mb (<a id="xref-ref-19-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-19">19</a>) and the estimated genome size of the reference accession Col-0 from flow cytometry is 166 Mb (<a id="xref-ref-20-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-20">20</a>)], the <em>C. himalaica</em> genome is considerably larger. It has been well documented that polyploidization (whole-genome duplication, WGD) events and transposable element (TE) amplification are major causes of genome expansion (<a id="xref-ref-21-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-21">21</a>, <a id="xref-ref-22-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-22">22</a>). Analyses of age distributions built from transversion substitutions at fourfold degenerate sites (4DTv) indicated that, except for the α (4DTv distance = ∼0.3) and β (4DTv distance = ∼0.6) polyploidy events shared among members of the Brassicaceae (<a id="xref-ref-23-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-23">23</a>), <em>C. himalaica</em> has not undergone an additional species-specific WGD event (<a id="xref-fig-2-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2<em>A</em></a>). Next, we investigated the content and evolutionary history of TEs in <em>C. himalaica</em>. Using de novo prediction of TEs (<a id="xref-sec-9-1" class="xref-sec" href="#sec-9"><em>Materials and Methods</em></a>), we identified and marked 46.91% of the assembly as repeat regions, among which TEs occupied 45.78% of the genome assembly length (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1817580116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S10</a>), which is higher than that reported for <em>A. lyrata</em> and <em>A. thaliana</em> (29.7% and 23.7%, respectively) (<a id="xref-ref-24-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-24">24</a>). This was also apparent in a CIRCOS genomic comparison plot, which showed that the density of TEs in <em>C. himalaica</em> was higher than that in <em>A. thaliana</em> or <em>C. rubella</em>, whereas the density of genes in <em>C. himalaica</em> was lower than that in <em>A. thaliana</em> or <em>C. rubella</em> (<a id="xref-fig-1-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1</a>).</p><div id="F2" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/14/7137/F2.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Evolutionary analyses of the C. himalaica genome. (A) Age distribution of 4DTv distance values between orthologs of C. himalaica and A. thaliana, C. himalaica and A. lyrata, and C. himalaica and C. rubella. (B) Insertion time distribution of LTR retrotransposons. (C) Estimation of divergence times of nine species in the Brassicaceae. (D) Venn diagram showing unique and shared gene families between genomes of C. himalaica and four close relatives." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-1204342201" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Evolutionary analyses of the &lt;em&gt;C. himalaica&lt;/em&gt; genome. (&lt;em&gt;A&lt;/em&gt;) Age distribution of 4DTv distance values between orthologs of &lt;em&gt;C. himalaica&lt;/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;A. thaliana&lt;/em&gt;, &lt;em&gt;C. himalaica&lt;/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;A. lyrata&lt;/em&gt;, and &lt;em&gt;C. himalaica&lt;/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;C. rubella&lt;/em&gt;. (&lt;em&gt;B&lt;/em&gt;) Insertion time distribution of LTR retrotransposons. (&lt;em&gt;C&lt;/em&gt;) Estimation of divergence times of nine species in the Brassicaceae. (&lt;em&gt;D&lt;/em&gt;) Venn diagram showing unique and shared gene families between genomes of &lt;em&gt;C. himalaica&lt;/em&gt; and four close relatives.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. 2." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/14/7137/F2.medium.gif" width="440" height="325"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. 2." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/14/7137/F2.medium.gif" width="440" height="325"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/14/7137/F2.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. 2." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>
<li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/14/7137/F2.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li>
<li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/857355" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 2.</span> <p id="p-16" class="first-child">Evolutionary analyses of the <em>C. himalaica</em> genome. (<em>A</em>) Age distribution of 4DTv distance values between orthologs of <em>C. himalaica</em> and <em>A. thaliana</em>, <em>C. himalaica</em> and <em>A. lyrata</em>, and <em>C. himalaica</em> and <em>C. rubella</em>. (<em>B</em>) Insertion time distribution of LTR retrotransposons. (<em>C</em>) Estimation of divergence times of nine species in the Brassicaceae. (<em>D</em>) Venn diagram showing unique and shared gene families between genomes of <em>C. himalaica</em> and four close relatives.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-17">In particular, a high proportion of TEs in <em>C. himalaica</em> were LTR retrotransposons (30.37%), whereas other retrotransposons (short and long interspersed nuclear elements) only constituted 3.31% collectively (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1817580116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S10</a>). The retrotransposon proliferation might be responsible for the genome-size expansion in <em>C. himalaica</em>. To investigate the evolutionary dynamics of LTR retrotransposons, we estimated their insertion dates in four closely related species (<a id="xref-sec-9-2" class="xref-sec" href="#sec-9"><em>Materials and Methods</em></a>). Compared with <em>A. thaliana</em>, which shows a large number of microdeletions in noncoding DNA and transposons (<a id="xref-ref-24-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-24">24</a>), <em>A. lyrata</em> has a comparatively high proportion of recent insertions (<a id="xref-ref-18-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-18">18</a>) (<a id="xref-fig-2-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2<em>B</em></a>), possibly contributing to its larger genome size (207 Mb). The proliferation of LTR retrotransposons in <em>C. himalaica</em> (<a id="xref-fig-2-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2<em>B</em></a> and <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1817580116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S4 and Table S11</a>) peaks at ∼2.0 Mya, shortly after the dramatic elevational and climatic changes of the QTP between the late Miocene and late Pliocene (<a id="xref-ref-1-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1">1</a>, <a id="xref-ref-13-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-13">13</a>). The activity of TEs (including LTR retrotransposons) can lead to diverse genetic changes (e.g., chromosomal rearrangements and gene duplication, creation, and disruption) that may drive lineage-specific diversification and adaptation (<a id="xref-ref-25-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-25">25</a>, <a id="xref-ref-26-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-26">26</a>). We therefore hypothesize that the proliferation of LTR retrotransposons likely contributed to the diversification, speciation, and adaptation of <em>C. himalaica</em>, similar to the proliferation of <em>BARE</em>-1 retrotransposons in wild barley (<a id="xref-ref-27-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-27">27</a>) and <em>DIRS1</em> retrotransposons in Antarctic teleost (<a id="xref-ref-28-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">28</a>), which might have facilitated the adaptation of plants to drier slopes and subzero temperatures, respectively. Another example from apples involves a major burst of retrotransposons ∼21.0 Mya, which coincided with the uplift of the Tianshan mountains, the postulated center of origin of apples (<a id="xref-ref-29-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-29">29</a>).</p></div><div id="sec-4" class="subsection"><h3>Phylogenetic Tree Construction and Estimation of Divergence Times.</h3><p id="p-18">Previous studies suggest that <em>Crucihimalaya</em> forms an endemic genus in the Himalayas and is most closely related to <em>Arabidopsis</em> (<a id="xref-ref-30-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-30">30</a>, <a id="xref-ref-31-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-31">31</a>). However, our earlier phylogenetic analyses of transcriptome sequences found that <em>C. himalaica</em> formed a clade sister to <em>C. rubella</em> (<a id="xref-ref-4-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4">4</a>). Applying OrthoMCL (<a id="xref-ref-32-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">32</a>) to nine published whole-genome sequences from Brassicaceae (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1817580116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S12</a>), we identified a total of 22,670 orthogroups. Among these orthogroups, 4,586 contained putative single-copy gene families. To verify the phylogenetic position of <em>C. himalaica</em>, we generated a maximum likelihood phylogenetic tree with a trimmed and concatenated protein sequence alignment from 4,586 single-copy genes in nine species. The resulting phylogeny indicated that <em>C. himalaica</em> was most closely related to <em>C. rubella</em>, and that these two species in turn formed a clade with <em>A. lyrata</em> and <em>A. thaliana</em> (<a id="xref-fig-2-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2<em>C</em></a>), confirming our previous results (<a id="xref-ref-4-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4">4</a>)<sub>.</sub> The above-mentioned four genera (which are classified in the tribe Camelineae), together with the allied <em>Cardamine hirsuta</em>, were often recognized as Lineage I or Clade A in previous phylogenetic studies (<a id="xref-ref-33-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-33">33</a>, <a id="xref-ref-34-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-34">34</a>).</p><p id="p-19"><em>C. himalaica</em> and <em>C. rubella</em> were estimated to have diverged ∼10.6 (8.8 to 12.2) Mya in our analyses using MCMCtree (<a id="xref-ref-35-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-35">35</a>) with three calibration points (<a id="xref-sec-9-3" class="xref-sec" href="#sec-9"><em>Materials and Methods</em></a> and <a id="xref-fig-2-5" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2<em>C</em></a>); the two species diverged from <em>Arabidopsis</em> ∼14.6 (12.7 to 17.2) Mya and from <em>Brassica rapa</em> ∼23.6 (20.9 to 27.8) Mya. These results are in agreement with previous estimates (<a id="xref-ref-11-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11">11</a>, <a id="xref-ref-36-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-36">36</a>). Given the absence of genomic information for the remaining species of <em>Crucihimalaya</em>, we are unable to date the exact origin of <em>C. himalaica</em>, which was estimated previously at ∼3.56 Mya (<a id="xref-ref-11-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11">11</a>, <a id="xref-ref-12-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-12">12</a>). However, <em>C. himalaica</em> must have evolved after the split with <em>Capsella</em> and almost certainly less than 10.6 Mya. Such estimation is in accordance with the timing of the most recent rapid uplift of the QTP from late Miocene to late Pliocene (<a id="xref-ref-1-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1">1</a>, <a id="xref-ref-13-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-13">13</a>). The genus <em>Crucihimalaya</em> is assigned to the tribe Crucihimalayeae, which includes two additional genera, <em>Ladakiella</em> and <em>Transberingia</em> (<a id="xref-ref-37-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-37">37</a>, <a id="xref-ref-38-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-38">38</a>). The tribe Crucihimalayeae is mainly distributed in Central Asia, with one species (<em>Transberingia bursifolia</em>) extending into North America. Therefore, we speculate that the ancestor of <em>C. himalaica</em> dispersed from Central Asia into the Himalayas, and speciation of <em>C. himalaica</em> was likely triggered by the rapid uplift of the Himalayas, which resulted in the evolution of specialized phenotypic and physiological characters as adaptations to the extreme environment (discussed further below).</p></div><div id="sec-5" class="subsection"><h3>Gene Family Expansion and Contraction.</h3><p id="p-20">Significant expansion or contraction in the size of particular gene families is often associated with the adaptive divergence of closely related species (<a id="xref-ref-39-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-39">39</a>, <a id="xref-ref-40-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-40">40</a>). Comparisons of the genomes of <em>C. himalaica</em> and four close relatives (<a id="xref-fig-2-6" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2<em>D</em></a>) identified a total of 151 gene families that are significantly (<em>P</em> &lt; 0.01) expanded in <em>C. himalaica</em> and 89 gene families that are significantly contracted (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1817580116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S13</a>). Based on Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes (KEGG) and Gene Ontology (GO) annotations, expanded gene families were highly enriched in ubiquitin-conjugating enzyme (E2) activity (<em>P</em> = 7.17E-40) and DNA repair pathways (<em>P</em> = 1.45E-12) (<a id="xref-table-wrap-2-1" class="xref-table" href="#T2">Table 2</a>). It is notable that the most significantly contracted gene families in the <em>C. himalaica</em> genome were found to be functionally related to disease and immune responses, such as the Toll-like receptor and NF-κB signaling pathway (<a id="xref-table-wrap-2-2" class="xref-table" href="#T2">Table 2</a>). The majority of bacteria interact with Toll-like receptors on the surface of the host cell membrane, stimulating the NF-κB signaling pathway in the immune response (<a id="xref-ref-41-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-41">41</a>). The Toll and interleukin-1 receptor (TIR) is an N-terminal component of the nucleotide-binding site (NBS) disease resistance protein family, which includes the TIR-NBS-LRR (TNL) and CC-NBS-LRR (CNL) subfamilies. In the <em>C. himalaica</em> genome, both TNL and CNL subfamilies underwent severe contractions compared with their close relatives (<a id="xref-fig-3-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3<em>A</em></a>). In particular, disease resistance RPP8-like proteins (belonging to CNL) have undergone the most significant contraction in the <em>C. himalaica</em> genome (6 vs. 16∼29 members, <em>P</em> = 1.26E-10). These proteins are major players in plant defense against pathogens by triggering hypersensitive responses (<a id="xref-ref-42-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-42">42</a>). The rapid evolutionary expansion or contraction of the NBS gene family may be a fundamental strategy for plants to adapt to the rapidly changing species-specific pathogen spectrum (<a id="xref-ref-43-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-43">43</a>, <a id="xref-ref-44-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-44">44</a>). As fewer microorganisms exist on the QTP owing to the harsh environments characterized by cold temperatures, aridity, and high UV radiation (<a id="xref-ref-45-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-45">45</a>), it is reasonable to presume that the reduction in number of NBS genes in the <em>C. himalaica</em> genome is due to a lighter load of pathogens present in the environment and therefore the scarcity of pathogen infection on the QTP. We performed additional searches for NBS pseudogenes, which were assumed to have frameshift mutations and/or premature stop codons. Only one pseudogene could be found in <em>A. thaliana</em> and <em>A. lyrata</em>, but 20, 15, and 7 NBS pseudogenes could be identified in the <em>C. himalaica</em>, <em>C. rubella</em>, and <em>C. hirsuta</em> genomes, respectively (<a id="xref-fig-3-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3<em>B</em></a>). Therefore, pseudogenization of NBS disease resistance genes in <em>C. himalaica</em> is at least partly responsible for the contraction of this gene family. Similar observations were made for the genome of the ground tit (<em>Parus humilis</em>), native to the QTP, where MHC genes involved in the cellular immune defense against pathogens also show significant contractions (<a id="xref-ref-46-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-46">46</a>). Although the mechanism of MHC gene evolution in ground tit remains unclear, it is worth noting that both ground tit and <em>C. himalaica</em> show a convergent evolution with respect to the contraction of gene families involved in defense against pathogens.</p><div id="T2" class="table pos-float"><div class="table-inline table-callout-links"><div class="callout"><span>View this table:</span><ul class="callout-links"><li class="view-inline first"><a href="##" class="table-expand-inline" data-table-url="/highwire/markup/857339/expansion?postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed&amp;table-expand-inline=1" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View inline</a></li>
<li class="view-popup last"><a href="/highwire/markup/857339/expansion?width=1000&amp;height=500&amp;iframe=true&amp;postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed" class="colorbox colorbox-load table-expand-popup" rel="gallery-fragment-tables" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View popup</a></li>
</ul></div></div><div class="table-caption"><span class="table-label">Table 2.</span> <p id="p-21" class="first-child">Functional annotation of the most significantly expansive and contract gene families in <em>C. himalaica</em></p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><div id="F3" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/14/7137/F3.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Size of the NBS gene family (A) and number of pseudo-NBS genes (B) in the C. himalaica genome compared with those of related species." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-1204342201" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Size of the NBS gene family (&lt;em&gt;A&lt;/em&gt;) and number of pseudo-NBS genes (&lt;em&gt;B&lt;/em&gt;) in the &lt;em&gt;C. himalaica&lt;/em&gt; genome compared with those of related species.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. 3." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/14/7137/F3.medium.gif" width="440" height="171"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. 3." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/14/7137/F3.medium.gif" width="440" height="171"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/14/7137/F3.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. 3." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>
<li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/14/7137/F3.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li>
<li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/857301" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 3.</span> <p id="p-22" class="first-child">Size of the NBS gene family (<em>A</em>) and number of pseudo-NBS genes (<em>B</em>) in the <em>C. himalaica</em> genome compared with those of related species.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div><div id="sec-6" class="subsection"><h3>Positive Selection on Single-Copy Genes.</h3><p id="p-23">Orthologs that show signs of positive selection usually underwent adaptive divergence (<a id="xref-ref-47-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-47">47</a>). Previously, we observed that the ratio of nonsynonymous to synonymous substitutions (dN/dS or ω) in <em>C. himalaica</em> is higher than those of closely related species, suggestive of accelerated evolution in <em>C. himalaica</em> after divergence from its ancestral lineage (<a id="xref-ref-4-6" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4">4</a>). In the present study, we conducted a positive selection analysis using the genomic sequences of <em>C. himalaica</em> and four close relatives. Among the 21,383 orthogroups, 11,085 contained single-copy orthologous genes. We used the branch-site model of the PAML 4 package (<a id="xref-ref-35-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-35">35</a>) to identify genes with signs of positive selection. As a result, 844 genes possibly under positive selection were identified in the <em>C. himalaica</em> genome (ω &gt; 1, <em>P</em> &lt; 0.05). Of these genes, 610 showed highly significant (<em>P</em> &lt; 0.01) positive selection (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1817580116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S14</a>). A KEGG functional classification of the 610 significant PSGs in the <em>C. himalaica</em> genome (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1817580116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S15</a>) showed that several categories associated with DNA repair, the ubiquitin system, as well as plant hormone biosynthesis and signal transduction were enriched. Genes involved in DNA repair were also identified as being under positive selection pressure in a previous transcriptomic study of <em>C. himalaica</em> (<a id="xref-ref-4-7" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4">4</a>). It is notable that significantly expanded gene families and PSGs were both enriched in DNA repair and protein ubiquitination pathways. Signal transduction-related CheY-like genes were also found to have undergone both significant positive selection and expansion events in our previous study on the adaptive evolution of the cyanobacterium <em>Trichormus</em> sp. NMC-1 on the QTP (<a id="xref-ref-3-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-3">3</a>), again providing evidence that expansion/duplication and (subsequent) positive selection of genes are an important mechanism for plant adaptive evolution.</p><p id="p-24">The extremely intense UV radiation on the QTP may influence plant growth and developmental processes such as photoperiodism and flowering, or cause DNA, RNA, and protein damage (<a id="xref-ref-48-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-48">48</a>). Our results showed that 10 PSGs in the <em>C. himalaica</em> genome are involved in the DNA repair pathway (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1817580116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S15</a>). Notably, several genes in the nucleotide excision repair pathway showed signatures of positive selection. One of them encodes the DNA excision repair protein ERCC-1 (or UV hypersensitive 7), which was also identified previously (<a id="xref-ref-4-8" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4">4</a>) and is reported to function as a component of a structure-specific endonuclease that cleaves on the 5ʹ side of UV photoproducts in DNA (<a id="xref-ref-49-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-49">49</a>). In addition to ERCC-1, we identified PSGs that encode the AP endonuclease 2 protein (APEX2), DNA polymerase delta subunit 2 (POLD2), DNA mismatch repair protein MLH1, and DNA repair and recombination protein RAD54 (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1817580116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S14</a>). These PSGs are involved in base excision repair, mismatch excision repair, and homologous recombination, suggesting that <em>C. himalaica</em> has evolved an integrated DNA-repair mechanism to adapt to the harsh habitats caused by the uplift of the QTP. Moreover, high UV-B radiation is a common stress that both animals and plants on the QTP must cope with. The DNA repair and radiation responses pathways similarly have played crucial roles in the highland adaptation of the Tibetan highland barley (<a id="xref-ref-6-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-6">6</a>), Tibetan antelope (<a id="xref-ref-50-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-50">50</a>), Tibetan chicken (<a id="xref-ref-51-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-51">51</a>), and Tibetan hot-spring snake (<a id="xref-ref-52-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-52">52</a>).</p><p id="p-25">Our analyses also indicated that ubiquitin system-related gene families underwent significant expansion and natural selection. Ubiquitin-mediated proteolysis impacts almost every aspect of plant growth and development, including plant hormone signal transduction, photomorphogenesis, reproduction, and abiotic stress responses (<a id="xref-ref-53-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-53">53</a>). Such a multifunctional biological process is of even greater importance for plants in the complex harsh environment on the QTP. Among the seven PSGs in the ubiquitin system based on the KEGG annotation, for instance, one encodes an ortholog of the COP10 protein in <em>A. thaliana</em>, which can enhance the activity of ubiquitin-conjugating enzymes (E2s) (<a id="xref-ref-54-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-54">54</a>) and participates in light signal transduction and photomorphogenesis (<a id="xref-ref-55-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-55">55</a>). An additional PSG encodes the ortholog of the auxin transport protein BIG (E3 ubiquitin-protein ligase UBR4) in <em>A. thaliana</em>, which not only influences multiple auxin-mediated developmental processes (e.g., lateral root production and inflorescence architecture) but also plays a critical role in a multitude of light and phytohormone pathways (<a id="xref-ref-56-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-56">56</a>). These PSGs enriched in the ubiquitin system might be important for <em>Crucihimalaya</em> to better survive the harsh environment in the QTP. Similarly, in three mangrove species, the ubiquitin-mediated proteolysis pathway includes 12 genes that have experienced convergent evolution at conservative sites, presumably important for adaptation of mangroves to the harsh coastal habitat (<a id="xref-ref-57-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-57">57</a>).</p><p id="p-26">Moreover, many of the identified PSGs were associated with reproduction pathways (92 PSGs, <em>P</em> = 5.40E-06) based on GO annotation (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1817580116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S16</a>). One of the PSGs encodes the phytochrome and flowering time regulatory protein 1 (PFT1), which represses the PhyB-mediated light signaling and regulates the expression of FLOWERING LOCUS T (FT) and CONSTANS (CO) (<a id="xref-ref-58-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-58">58</a>, <a id="xref-ref-59-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-59">59</a>). In a low-red to far-red ratio environment, plants require CO and FT to fully accelerate flowering in long days (<a id="xref-ref-58-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-58">58</a>, <a id="xref-ref-59-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-59">59</a>). PFT1 acts as a hub to integrate a variety of interdependent environmental stimuli, including light quality and jasmonic acid-dependent defense responses. An additional gene encodes TERMINAL FLOWER 1 (TFL1), which controls inflorescence meristem identity and regulates flowering time in the long-day flowering pathway. As the QTP experiences a long sunshine duration and a short vegetation growing season, flowering time is particularly critical and affects both the life cycles and reproductive success of many alpine plants (<a id="xref-ref-60-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-60">60</a>). In the wild, <em>C. himalaica</em> initiates flowering relatively early (April). However, this species rarely blossoms in low-altitude areas outside the QTP. These observations suggest that <em>C. himalaica</em> has evolved specific reproductive strategies on photoperiodism and flowering-related pathways as an adaptation to the long sunshine duration and short growing season on the QTP.</p></div><div id="sec-7" class="subsection"><h3>S-Locus Structure and Self-Fertilization of <em>C. himalaica.</em></h3><p id="p-27">Self-incompatibility in Brassicaceae is controlled by the S-locus recognition system, which prevents self-fertilization (<a id="xref-ref-61-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-61">61</a>, <a id="xref-ref-62-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-62">62</a>). Whereas self-fertilization often leads to decreased fitness of homozygous offspring, it also ensures reproduction in absence of pollinators or suitable mates, and therefore can be advantageous for plants to expand their distribution edges and occupy new niches (<a id="xref-ref-63-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-63">63</a>, <a id="xref-ref-64-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-64">64</a>). The QTP is characterized by harsh conditions including a short growing season and low pollinator activities. Therefore, it is not surprising that <em>C. himalaica</em> is a selfing plant (<a id="xref-ref-9-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-9">9</a>). The <em>Arabidopsis</em>/<em>Capsella</em>-like S-locus contains the female specificity gene (S-receptor kinase, SRK) and the male specificity gene (S-locus cysteine-rich protein, SCR) (<a id="xref-ref-65-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-65">65</a>). Self-compatibility is achieved when the SRK receptor fails to recognize the SCR ligand, usually through a loss of SCR function (<a id="xref-ref-66-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-66">66</a><a id="xref-ref-67-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-67"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-68-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-68"><span>⇓</span></a>–<a id="xref-ref-69-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-69">69</a>). We manually annotated the S-locus flanking genes (<em>AKR3</em> and <em>U-box</em>) and the <em>SCR</em> gene on scaffold 29 of the <em>C. himalaica</em> genome assembly. Alignment of <em>Crucihimalaya SCR</em> coding sequences with homologs from miscellaneous <em>Arabidopsis halleri</em>, <em>A. lyrata</em>, <em>A. thaliana</em>, <em>Capsella grandiflora</em>, <em>C. rubella</em>, and <em>C. hirsuta</em> S-locus haplotypes showed that the closest haplotype to <em>C. himalaica</em> was the one from <em>A. halleri S15</em> (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1817580116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S5</a>) (<a id="xref-ref-66-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-66">66</a>, <a id="xref-ref-68-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-68">68</a>, <a id="xref-ref-70-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-70">70</a>, <a id="xref-ref-71-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-71">71</a>). Note that, due to strong balancing selection on the S-locus, highly diverged S-alleles are shared across species and <em>SCR</em> gene phylogeny does not follow species phylogeny (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1817580116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S5</a>) (<a id="xref-ref-72-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-72">72</a>, <a id="xref-ref-73-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-73">73</a>). SCR protein sequence alignment showed that <em>C. himalaica</em> appears to have lost two out of eight conserved cysteines (<a id="xref-fig-4-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4<em>A</em></a>) that are essential for structural and functional integrity of SCR ligand (<a id="xref-ref-66-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-66">66</a>, <a id="xref-ref-74-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-74">74</a>). This alone may explain the transition to self-fertilization in <em>C. himalaica.</em> Interestingly, neither <em>SRK</em> from the S15 haplogroup nor any other <em>SRK</em> had a BLAST hit to the <em>C. himalaica</em> genome (<a id="xref-fig-4-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4<em>B</em></a>). To investigate whether SRK is indeed lost from the genome, we mapped short reads (450-bp insert size library) of <em>C. himalaica</em> to the combination of the <em>C. himalaica</em> genome assembly and <em>A. halleri</em> S15 haplogroup of S-locus. The <em>SRK</em> gene region did not have any reads mapped, and therefore we conclude that <em>SRK</em> is not present in the <em>C. himalaica</em> genome and likely has been lost. Taken together, we reason that the transition to self-compatibility of <em>C. himalaica</em> was accomplished by both (<em>i</em>) loss of function in the male recognition gene <em>SCR</em> and (<em>ii</em>) loss of the female recognition gene <em>SRK</em>. It is often suggested that self-fertilization rates of alpine plants increase at higher altitudes (<a id="xref-ref-75-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-75">75</a>), due to the reduction in pollinator abundance and seed production in alpine plants (<a id="xref-ref-76-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-76">76</a>, <a id="xref-ref-77-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-77">77</a>). This suggestion is consistent with the fact that self-fertilizing populations also exist in <em>Arabis alpina,</em> a close relative of <em>C. himalaica</em> that grows at high altitudes (<a id="xref-ref-78-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-78">78</a>, <a id="xref-ref-79-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-79">79</a>). Moreover, previous studies also found that the rate of self-fertilizing hermaphroditic plants is increasing on the QTP and autonomous self-fertilization provides substantial reproductive assurance in the pollinator scarcity condition of the QTP (<a id="xref-ref-80-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-80">80</a>, <a id="xref-ref-81-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-81">81</a>). Therefore, the transition to a self-compatibility mating system in <em>C. himalaica</em> likely facilitated its occupation of the QTP.</p><div id="F4" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/14/7137/F4.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Transitioning to self-fertilization in C. himalaica. Cysteine residues are highlighted in red, showing eight conserved sites important for structural and functional integrity of the protein (<a id="xref-ref-66-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-66">66</a>, <a id="xref-ref-74-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-74">74</a>).
