(<em>B</em>) Structure of the S-locus part of scaffold 29 in <em>C. himalaica</em> genome assembly compared with <em>A. halleri</em> S15 haplogroup (<a id="xref-ref-70-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-70">70</a>). Colors of the lines between S-loci correspond to BLAST scores from highest (blue) to lowest (gray).</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div></div><div class="section conclusion" id="sec-8"><h2 class="">Conclusion</h2><p id="p-29" class="flushleft">Organisms that live on the QTP face a variety of abiotic stresses under the harsh environmental conditions and presumably have been subjected to a series of adaptive evolutionary changes. In the present study, we de novo-sequenced the genome of <em>C. himalaica</em>, a species exclusively found in the Himalayan region. Based on phylogenetic reconstruction and estimated divergence times, we propose that the speciation of <em>C. himalaica</em> was triggered by the uplift of the QTP, mediated by genomic evolution to adapt to the altered environment. The proliferation of LTR retrotransposons may at least partly be responsible for the increased genome size of <em>C. himalaica</em>. The significant contraction and pseudogenization of NBS gene families reflect the strong reduction in pathogen incidence on the QTP. Gene families that underwent significant expansion and genes that show signs of positive selection are enriched in DNA repair and protein ubiquitination pathways, which probably reflect to the adaptation of <em>C. himalaica</em> to high radiation, low temperature, and pathogen-depauperate environments on the QTP. Occupation of the QTP by <em>C. himalaica</em> was likely facilitated by self-compatibility, which, as we have shown here, involved both male and female components of the recognition system. Both similarities (e.g., DNA repair and disease-resistance pathways) and differences (e.g., reproduction and plant hormone-related pathways) in adaptive mechanisms have been identified among plants and animals that grow at high altitudes. Although further experimental verification is needed, our results provide insights into how plants adapt to harsh and extreme environments.</p></div><div class="section materials-methods" id="sec-9"><h2 class="">Materials and Methods</h2><div id="sec-10" class="subsection"><h3>Plant Material, Genome Sequencing, and Assembly.</h3><p id="p-30">Seedlings of <em>C. himalaica</em> were sampled from Batang County (altitude 4,010 m, N 30.313°, E 99.358°) of QTP. Seedlings from the same individual were cultivated in the greenhouse at Kunming Institute of Botany. High-quality genomic DNA was extracted using the Qiagen DNeasy Plant Mini Kit. The <em>C. himalaica</em> genome assembly was performed using sequence data obtained from a combination of sequencing technologies: Illumina PE reads, Illumina MP reads, and PacBio RS II reads (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1817580116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S2</a>). The whole step of library construction and sequencing was performed at Novogene Bioinformatics Technology Co., Ltd. First, six PE libraries were prepared to sequence the <em>C. himalaica</em> genome. These included two PE libraries with insert sizes of 250 and 450 bp and four MP libraries with insert sizes of 2, 5, 10 and 15 kb. Whole genomic sequence (44.49 Gb) data were generated solely using Illumina platforms (HiSEq. 2500) and assembled using ALLPATHS -LG (<a id="xref-ref-15-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-15">15</a>). Next, the PacBio reads (6.19 Gb) were used to fill gaps using the PBJelly2 tool (<a id="xref-ref-16-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-16">16</a>), followed by scaffold assembly with SSPACE (<a id="xref-ref-82-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-82">82</a>) using long-insert-size PE reads. The accuracy and completeness of the assemblies were assessed by aligning the reads from short-insert-size libraries back to the scaffolds using BWA-mem (v. 0.7.17) (<a id="xref-ref-83-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-83">83</a>).</p></div><div id="sec-11" class="subsection"><h3>Gene Prediction and Annotation.</h3><p id="p-31">Gene prediction was performed using a combination of homology, de novo, and transcriptome-based approaches. Gene models were integrated by EvidenceModeler (<a href="http://evidencemodeler.github.io/" class="in-nw">evidencemodeler.github.io/</a>). Gene models were further updated by PASA (<a id="xref-ref-84-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-84">84</a>) to generate UTRs and provide information on alternative splicing variants. The predicted genes were analyzed for functional domains and homologs using InterProScan and BLAST against the NCBI nonredundant protein sequence database, TrEMBL and SwissProt with an E-value cutoff 1E-15 and Blast2GO with default parameters. Completeness of the genome was also assessed by performing core gene annotation using the BUSCO (<a id="xref-ref-17-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17">17</a>) methods. Transcription factors were identified and classified into different families using the iTAK pipeline (<a href="http://bioinfo.bti.cornell.edu/cgi-bin/itak/index.cgi" class="in-nw">bioinfo.bti.cornell.edu/cgi-bin/itak/index.cgi</a>) (<a id="xref-ref-85-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-85">85</a>).</p></div><div id="sec-12" class="subsection"><h3>Repetitive Elements Identification and Dynamic Analysis.</h3><p id="p-32">A combined strategy based on homologous sequence alignment and de novo searches was used to identify repeat elements in the <em>C. himalaica</em> genome. We de novo predicted TEs using RepeatModeler (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler" class="in-nw">www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler</a>), RepeatScout (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/" class="in-nw">www.repeatmasker.org</a>), Piler (<a id="xref-ref-86-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-86">86</a>) (<a href="http://www.drive5.com/piler/" class="in-nw">www.drive5.com/piler/</a>), and LTR-Finder (<a id="xref-ref-87-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-87">87</a>) (<a href="http://tlife.fudan.edu.cn/tlife/ltr_finder" class="in-nw">tlife.fudan.edu.cn/tlife/ltr_finder</a>) with default parameters. For alignment of homologous sequences to identify repeats in the assembled genome, we used RepeatProteinMask and RepeatMasker (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/" class="in-nw">www.repeatmasker.org</a>) with the repbase library (<a id="xref-ref-88-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-88">88</a>). TEs overlapping with the same type of repeats were integrated, while those with low scores were removed if they overlapped over 80% of their lengths and belonged to different types.</p><p id="p-33">Intact LTR retrotransposons were identified by searching the genomes of <em>C. himalaica</em> with LTRharvest (<a id="xref-ref-89-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-89">89</a>) (-motif tgca -motifmis 1) and LTR_Finder (LTR length 100 to 5,000 nt; length between two LTRs: 1,000 to 2,0000 nt). We combined results from both analyses after integrating the overlap sites. Candidate sequences were filtered by a two-step procedure to reduce false positives. First, LTRdigest (<a id="xref-ref-90-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-90">90</a>) was used to identify the primer binding site (PBS) motif, and only elements containing PBS were retained; then, protein domains (pol, gag, and env) in candidate LTR retrotransposons were identified by searching against HMM profiles considered as intact. Second, clustering of candidate LTR retrotransposons was performed using the SiLiX software package (<a href="http://lbbe.univ-lyon1.fr/SiLiX" class="in-nw">lbbe.univ-lyon1.fr/SiLiX</a>). Using a substitution rate (r) of 7*10<sup>−9</sup> substitutions per site per year (<a id="xref-ref-91-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-91">91</a>, <a id="xref-ref-92-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-92">92</a>), the insertion date (T) can be calculated for each LTR retrotransposons (T = K/2r, K: genetic distance). Tandemly repeated gene arrays were identified using BLASTP with a threshold E value &lt; 10<sup>−8</sup>; fragmental alignments were conjoined for each gene pair. The tandem repeat gene clusters were identified using a cutoff of sequence identity &gt;70% and distance &lt;150 kb. Each cluster with more than two genes was retained.</p></div><div id="sec-13" class="subsection"><h3>Whole-Genome Alignment and Duplication Analysis.</h3><p id="p-34">We aligned the <em>C. himalaica</em> genome to those of <em>A. thaliana, A. lyrata</em>, and <em>C. rubella</em> using LASTZ (<a id="xref-ref-93-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-93">93</a>) with the following parameter values: M = 254 K = 4500 L = 3000 Y = 15000 -seed = match 12 -step = 20 -identity = 85. To avoid the interference caused by repetitive sequences for sequence alignment, RepeatMasker and RepBase library were used to mask repetitive sequences of the above four genomes. The raw alignments were combined into larger blocks using the ChainNet algorithm. We identified orthologous genes among the <em>C. himalaica</em>, <em>A. thaliana</em>, <em>A. lyrata</em>, and <em>C. rubella</em> genomes and paralogous genes within <em>C. himalaica</em> and other relatives using BLASTP (E value &lt; 1E-7). MCscanx (<a id="xref-ref-94-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-94">94</a>) was used to identify syntenic blocks within the genome. For each gene pair in a syntenic block, the 4DTv distance was calculated; values of all gene pairs were plotted to identify putative whole-genome duplication events and divergence in two species.</p></div><div id="sec-14" class="subsection"><h3>Phylogenetic Tree Construction and Estimation of Species Divergence Times.</h3><p id="p-35">We selected genomes of <em>C. himalaica</em> and eight other species (<em>A. thaliana</em>, <em>A. lyrata</em>, <em>C. rubella</em>, <em>C. hirsuta</em>, <em>Eutrema salsugineum</em>, <em>B. rapa</em>, <em>Schrenkiella parvula</em>, and <em>Aethionema arabicum</em>) to identify orthologs. To remove redundancy caused by alternative splicing variations, we retained only gene models at each gene locus that encoded the longest protein sequence. To exclude putative fragmented genes, genes encoding protein sequences shorter than 50 aa were filtered out. All filtered protein sequences of these plants were compared with each other using BLASTP (E value &lt; 1E-7) and clustered into orthologous groups using OrthoMCL (inflation parameter, 1.5) (<a id="xref-ref-33-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-33">33</a>). Protein sequences from 4,586 single-copy gene families were used for phylogenetic tree construction. MUSCLE (<a id="xref-ref-95-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-95">95</a>) was used to generate multiple sequence alignment for protein sequences in each single-copy family with default parameters. The alignments of each family were concatenated to a super alignment matrix, which was then used for phylogenetic tree reconstruction through the PROTCATJTT model in RAxML software.</p><p id="p-36">Divergence time between nine species was estimated using MCMCtree in PAML (<a id="xref-ref-35-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-35">35</a>) with the options “independent rates” and “GTR” model. A Markov chain Monte Carlo analysis was run for 10,000 generations, using a burn-in of 1,000 iterations. Three calibration points were applied based on a recent study using 53 plastomes of Brassicales: <em>A. arabicum</em> and other crucifers divergence time (29.0 to 41.8 Mya), core Brassicaceae origination time (21.3 to 29.8 Mya), and core <em>Arabidopsis</em> origination time (4.8 to 9.7 Mya) (<a id="xref-ref-36-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-36">36</a>).</p></div><div id="sec-15" class="subsection"><h3>Gene Family Expansion and Contraction.</h3><p id="p-37">We selected four close relatives (<em>A. thaliana</em>, <em>A</em>. <em>lyrata</em>, <em>C. rubella</em>, and <em>C. hirsuta</em>) of <em>C. himalaica</em> to identify orthogroups (gene families). Expansions and contractions of orthologous gene families were determined using CAFÉ v. 4.1 (<a id="xref-ref-96-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-96">96</a>). The program uses a birth and death process to model gene gain and loss over a phylogeny. For each significantly expanded and contracted gene family in <em>C. himalaica</em>, functional information was inferred based on its ortholog in <em>A. thaliana</em>. GO enrichment analyses of genes were conducted using web-based agriGO (<a href="http://systemsbiology.cau.edu.cn/agriGOv2" class="in-nw">systemsbiology.cau.edu.cn/agriGOv2</a>) (<a id="xref-ref-97-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-97">97</a>) with the singular enrichment analysis method and TAIR10 database. The KOBAS and BlastKOALA software (<a id="xref-ref-98-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-98">98</a>) was also used to test the statistical enrichment of genes in KEGG pathways (<a id="xref-ref-99-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-99">99</a>).</p></div><div id="sec-16" class="subsection"><h3>Identification and Classification of Putative NBS Resistance Genes.</h3><p id="p-38">A hidden Markov model search (<a href="http://hmmer.janelia.org/" class="in-nw">hmmer.janelia.org</a>) was used to identify NBS-encoding R genes in the <em>C. himalaica</em> genome and four close relatives (<em>A. thaliana</em>, <em>A. lyrata</em>, <em>C. rubella</em>, and <em>C. hirsuta</em>). The sequences were screened using HMMs to search for the Pfam NBS (NB-ARC) family PF00931 domain (<a href="http://pfam.xfam.org/" class="in-nw">pfam.xfam.org/</a>). Pfam HMM searches were performed using a TIR model (PF01582) and several LRR models (PF00560, PF07723, PF07725, PF12799, PF13306, PF13516, PF13504, PF13855, and PF08263) to detect TIR domains and LRR motifs in the NBS-encoding amino acid sequences. CC motifs were detected using the COILS prediction program 2.2 (<a href="http://embnet.vital-it.ch/software/COILS_form.html" class="in-nw">embnet.vital-it.ch/software/COILS_form.html</a>) with a <em>p</em>-score cutoff of 0.9.</p></div><div id="sec-17" class="subsection"><h3>Gene Family-Based Positive Selection Tests.</h3><p id="p-39">For positive selection analyses we also selected the four most closely related species with assembled genomes (<em>A. thaliana</em>, <em>A</em>. <em>lyrata</em>, <em>C. rubella</em>, and <em>C. hirsuta</em>) and <em>C. himalaica</em> to identify orthologs based on our phylogenetic tree. The analysis procedure is similar to the phylogenetic tree analysis. First, we used OrthoMCL (<a id="xref-ref-32-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">32</a>) to identify homologous gene clusters (orthogroups) among the five genomes. OrthoMCL was run with an E-value cutoff of 1E-15 and an inflation parameter of 1.5 due to the close genetic relationship between five relatives. Orthogroups with single-copy genes shared by all five genomes were retained for further analyses. Multiple sequence alignment was performed for each orthogroup using MUSCLE v. 3.8.31 (<a id="xref-ref-97-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-97">97</a>) with default parameters. Poorly aligned regions were further trimmed using the trimAl v. 1.4 software (<a id="xref-ref-100-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-100">100</a>) with the parameter “-gt 0.8 -st 0.001”. Maximum likelihood trees were generated using RAxML v7.0.4 (<a id="xref-ref-101-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-101">101</a>) with the PROTCATJTT model. To calculate the nonsynonymous (<em>K</em><sub>a</sub>) and synonymous (Ks) substitution rates between pairs of orthogroups, we reverse-translated amino acid alignments to the corresponding codon-based nucleotide alignments using PAL2NAL (<a id="xref-ref-102-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-102">102</a>). To increase the power of tests for positive selection, we applied the branch-site model (<a id="xref-ref-103-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-103">103</a>) implemented in codeml program (<a id="xref-ref-35-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-35">35</a>) to estimate the dN/dS substitution rates (ω value). We also deleted all gaps (clean data = 1) from the alignments to lower the effect of ambiguous bases on the inference of positive selection. A foreground branch was specified as the clade of <em>C. himalaica</em>. A likelihood ratio test was conducted to determine whether positive selection is operating in the foreground branch. In this study, PSGs were inferred if the <em>P</em> value was less than 0.01. The functional annotation of PSGs in <em>C. himalaica</em> was also conducted using the same approach and the same <em>P-</em>value cutoff with the gene family expansion and contraction analysis.</p></div><div id="sec-18" class="subsection"><h3>S-Locus Structure and Self-Fertilization of <em>C. himalaica.</em></h3><p id="p-40">Known S-haplogroups from <em>Arabidopsis</em> and <em>Capsella</em> were used as query to search the genome assembly of <em>C. himalaica</em> (<a id="xref-ref-70-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-70">70</a>, <a id="xref-ref-104-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-104">104</a>) using BLASTP. We manually annotated both flanking genes of the S-locus on scaffold 29: the <em>ARK3</em> gene and <em>U-box</em> gene. However, no homologs were detected in our search for the <em>SRK</em> gene. Using the standard BWA-mem (v. 0.7.17) (<a id="xref-ref-83-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-83">83</a>) and Samtools (v. 1.6) (<a id="xref-ref-105-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-105">105</a>) pipeline, we mapped short reads (450-bp insert size library) of <em>C. himalaica</em> to the combined reference of <em>C. himalaica</em> genome and <em>A. halleri</em> S15 haplogroup of S-locus (<a id="xref-ref-70-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-70">70</a>) and then manually explored the coverage of the <em>SRK</em> region of the S15 haplogroup using IGV (v. 2.4.13) (<a id="xref-ref-106-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-106">106</a>). The coding sequence of the predicted <em>SCR</em> gene (ID = AT4G22105.1_Ath-D2 on scaffold29:1034290-1034749), together with SCR sequences from <em>A. thaliana</em>, <em>C. rubella</em>, <em>C. grandiflora</em>, and <em>C. hirsuta,</em> was added to the curated alignment of the publicly available SCR genes kindly provided by Vincent Castric (Unité Evo-Eco-Paléo, CNRS/Université de Lille 1, Villeneuve d’Ascq, France) from <em>A. halleri</em> and <em>A. lyrata</em> using MUSCLE (v. 3.8.31) (<a id="xref-ref-97-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-97">97</a>), and a maximum likelihood tree with 500 bootstrap replicates was constructed using MEGAX (<a id="xref-ref-107-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-107">107</a>) and visualized using Jalview2 (<a id="xref-ref-108-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-108">108</a>). A comparative structure plot of <em>C. himalaica</em> and <em>A. halleri</em> S15 S-loci (<a id="xref-fig-4-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4<em>B</em></a>) was constructed using the R library genoPlotR (<a id="xref-ref-109-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-109">109</a>).</p></div></div><div class="section ack" id="ack-1"><h2>Acknowledgments</h2><p id="p-41">We dearly cherish the memory of our respected mentor and friend Prof. Yang Zhong at Fudan University for his full support to this project. We thank Takashi Tsuchimatsu and Vincent Castric for their feedback on the S-locus part. This work was supported by National Natural Science Foundation of China Grants 31770408, 31590823, 31760082, 31760127, U1802232, and 91131901 (to T.Z., H.S., Q.Q., and L.Q.), National Key R &amp; D Program of China Grant 2017YF0505200 (to H.S.), the Strategic Priority Research Program of Chinese Academy of Sciences Grant XDA 20050203 (to H.S.), National High Technology Research and Development Program of China Grant 2014AA020528 (to T.Z.), the Chinese Academy of Sciences “Light of West China” Program (J.H.), and European Union Seventh Framework Programme Grant FP7/2007-2013 under European Research Council Advanced Grant Agreement 322739 – DOUBLEUP (to Y.V.d.P.). P.Y.N. is a postdoctoral fellow of the Research Foundation–Flanders (12S9618N).</p></div><div class="section fn-group" id="fn-group-1"><h2>Footnotes</h2><ul class="pnas-footnotes"><li class="fn-equal" id="fn-4"><p id="p-4"><a class="rev-xref" href="#xref-fn-4-1">↵</a><sup>1</sup>T.Z., Q.Q., and P.Y.N. contributed equally to this work.</p></li><li class="corresp" id="corresp-1"><a class="rev-xref" href="#xref-corresp-1-1">↵</a><sup>2</sup>To whom correspondence may be addressed.         <h2 class="pane-title"><span class="pane-title-text">Article Classifications</span></h2>
    
  
  <div class="pane-content">
